More than any of the other MCU-Flicks, this feels like something made by (a confused) committee, further mutilated in post-production. Aside from Iman Vellani (in front of the camera) and Laura Karpman (who wrote the, admittedly, fairly entertaining Score) no one involved in this seemed to really have fun doing it. It feels unfinished, stitched together and half-assed. And doesn't look anything near its gigantic budget. There are a few decent fight scenes built around the gimmick that the three change places when they use their powers, Sam Jackson is reliable as always, the scene where they evacuate the Station using the Flerkens is kinda hilarious in a messed up kind of way and Iman Vellani is likeable (as is her characters' family). Aside from that, not much positive to say, I'm afraid. This is not the worst MCU-related thing released in recent years, but I think it represents the franchise at its low point because it just seemed tired.

SPOILERS:  This movie could've been good and it did have some good ideas. I liked the idea of how Captain Marvel's actions in the first movie fucked over the Cree world. They should've explored this more.  I think this movie should've been about identity crisis and also imposter syndrome. In the first movie, Captain Marvel uses her powers and therefore identity to "save the day" only to realize she screwed over the Cree world. Not only this, but Captain Marvel literally can't recall her past and who she was before. Her being a hero also affected her relationship with Rambo. They should've explored these things in this movie a lot more and have her really struggle with who she is, and if what she is is good (similar to Tobey Maguire's Spiderman 2).  At the same time, Captain Marvel is also one of the strongest avengers yet she messes up by screwing the Cree world over, and also not being able to save Rambo. Should've explored this more and had her question if she's good enough. Then at the end/climax, have her tie the identity crisis and imposter syndrome into accepting who she is currently and her finding her own voice. They could even tie this to feminism in a smart way.

So I actually saw this review when deciding whether or not to see the Marvels in theaters, and obviously this swayed me in the opposite direction. I wasn't a fan of the Captain Marvel movie and thought Ms. Marvel was just passable. I have to say I'm disappointed in this review after watching it on Disney+. Is The Marvels the best MCU movie? Not by a long shot, but the plot and acting was a lot better than you gave it credit for. I was honestly expecting a hot mess of a movie with a patchwork plot, but instead I got a pretty heartfelt movie with some funny moments and pretty good fights. I know MCU fatigue is a thing, but that doesn't mean we need to discount every film that's not a certified masterpiece as terrible. While I do feel the villain could have been more fleshed out and the Kree could have used a bit more filling, that's really the only downside to this movie (if you can call it that). It's solid, it sucks that you kind of need to know the backstory of the Captain Marvel movie and Wandavision to understand how we got to this point and why we should care, but that's kind of how these MCU movies roll ever since Phase 2. While there have been a couple movies that have really shat the bed this Phase, I'm not counting this as one of them. Just lighten up and let the movie play out, and you'll end up having a good time.

As a public service, I'm going to explain what the deal is with the swapping.  Have you noticed that things in MCU movies kinda sorta refer to something in the comics but is not quite the same? Well, the first, male Captain Marvel got his powers from the Nega-bands (referenced in Kamala's bracelets) but due to cosmic shenanigans those bands got in the hands of one Rick Jones. Since then, when Rick slammed them together, he would switch places with Captain Marvel. The active person would be in the physical universe, the other one would go to a pocket dimension. By the way, they could still talk to each other.  So the swapping places in The Marvels is something of a reference to that dynamic, in a more crippling, three superpowered stooges kind of way.

It‚Äôs more believable that a scientist can change into the Hulk than Wakanda somehow became the richest, most technologically advanced country on the planet without ANY trading from other countries. How‚Äôd they get their money and from who. How‚Äôd they get their education? It takes first world money to get a first world education. Having a wealth of precious metal doesn‚Äôt matter if you keep it to yourself. Imagine if the Middle East just kept all its oil to itself. Imagine how worse off they would be. My point is, the entire concept of the Black Panther and Wakanda drips of white guilt. Consider the source. The writer and the illustrator are both white guys in the prime of their lives during the civil rights movement and created Black Panther in the mid-60s. And he ‚Äújust happens‚Äù to be a king of a secret super rich, super advanced African country. Of course, when we all think of technological powerhouse countries, African nations jump to the top of our lists. Oh, that‚Äôs right, they don‚Äôt. Unless you feel bad about being a Caucasian during civil rights, in which case central Africa is the perfect spot for such a place. T‚ÄôChalla‚Äôs arguably the most complete Avenger. He‚Äôs the richest, he‚Äôs as athletic as Captain America thanks to his Black Panther powers, he‚Äôs got at least as many fighting skills as Black Widow, he‚Äôs a master of every weapon known to man, he may even be the smartest Avenger on a team that includes Bruce Banner and Tony Stark. His character feels thirsty to be considered great, almost to the point of being blaxploitative. Spider-Man exploits the culture of living in NYC but at least he still feels vulnerable and beatable. But not Black Panther. No, he‚Äôs so strong and complete a hero that the only thing that can possibly match him is ANOTHER Black Panther. Though this is forgivable and predictable seeing as how all Avengers fight mirror images of themselves. But why exactly should we root for him and/or feel bad for him? When some of the most fair and impartial reviewers on the platform says your movie is shit, then you really have to stop doing your BullSHIT.
One of the issues is how heavily the post-Endgame MCU leaned into two concepts: the Multiverse and the Kree.  One of the advantages of early Avengers movies was that, because they were origin stories, they could add high-concept stuff on the margins of the films.  That way, if something didn't work out, they could just ignore it or disregard it later.  Odin's treasure vault already had an Infinity Gauntlet?  Fake.  Nick Fury's suggesting Tony Stark thought he was the only superhero in town?  Immediately disregarded when it turned out that his dad worked with the original SHIELD and Captain America.  It was a huge advantage, because if something didn't work or fit, they could just ignore it, and it gave them room to play around with new concepts on the margins and disregard it later if needed.  Now though?  All their high-concept stuff has to be front and center.  As a result, if an idea doesn't work or isn't well-received, it's a lot harder to just drop it or dismiss it.  Loki is basically doing an entire second season of a tv show right now to figure out how to give Marvel the option to drop the "multiverse" gimmick since the third Antman movie showed the idea didn't have quite the mileage they were hoping for...
